<h1 class="firstHeading mw-first-heading" id="firstHeading"><span class="mw-page-title-main">Allergy</span></h1>
<div class="vector-body" id="bodyContent">
<div class="noprint" id="siteSub">From Wikipedia, the free encyclopedia</div>
<div id="contentSub"></div>
<div id="contentSub2"></div>
<div id="jump-to-nav"></div>
<a class="mw-jump-link" href="#mw-head">Jump to navigation</a>
<a class="mw-jump-link" href="#searchInput">Jump to search</a>
<div class="mw-body-content mw-content-ltr" dir="ltr" id="mw-content-text" lang="en"><div class="mw-parser-output"><div class="shortdescription nomobile noexcerpt noprint searchaux" style="display:none">Immune system response to a substance that most people tolerate well</div>
<style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r1033289096">.mw-parser-output .hatnote{font-style:italic}.mw-parser-output div.hatnote{padding-left:1.6em;margin-bottom:0.5em}.mw-parser-output .hatnote i{font-style:normal}.mw-parser-output .hatnote+link+.hatnote{margin-top:-0.5em}</style><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">For the journal, see <a href="/wiki/Allergy_(journal)" title="Allergy (journal)">Allergy (journal)</a>.</div>
<p class="mw-empty-elt">
</p>
<div class="shortdescription nomobile noexcerpt noprint searchaux" style="display:none">Medical condition</div><style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r1066479718">.mw-parser-output .infobox-subbox{padding:0;border:none;margin:-3px;width:auto;min-width:100%;font-size:100%;clear:none;float:none;background-color:transparent}.mw-parser-output .mw-parser-output .infobox-full-data,body.skin-minerva .mw-parser-output .infobox-below{text-align:center}</style><table class="infobox"><tbody><tr><th class="infobox-above" colspan="2" swelling, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sneezing" title="Sneezing">sneezing</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Cough" title="Cough">cough</a>.</td></tr><tr><th class="infobox-label" href="/wiki/Anaphylaxis" title="Anaphylaxis">anaphylaxis</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NIH2015Types_1-0"><a href="#cite_note-NIH2015Types-1">[1]</a></sup></td></tr><tr><th class="infobox-label" scope="row">Causes</th><td class="infobox-data"><a href="/wiki/Genetics" title="Genetics">Genetic</a> and environmental factors<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Kay2000_2-0"><a href="#cite_note-Kay2000-2">[2]</a></sup></td></tr><tr><th class="infobox-label" scope="row"><a href="/wiki/Medical_diagnosis" title="Medical diagnosis">Diagnostic method</a></th><td class="infobox-data">Based on symptoms, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Skin_prick_test" title="Skin prick test">skin prick test</a>, <a href="/wiki/Blood_test" title="Blood test">blood test</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NIH2012pdf_3-0"><a href="#cite_note-NIH2012pdf-3">[3]</a></sup></td></tr><tr><th class="infobox-label" scope="row"><a href="/wiki/Differential_diagnosis" title="Differential diagnosis">Differential diagnosis</a></th><td class="infobox-data"><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Food_intolerances" title="Food intolerances">Food intolerances</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Food_poisoning" title="Food poisoning">food poisoning</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Bah2012_4-0"><a href="#cite_note-Bah2012-4">[4]</a></sup></td></tr><tr><th class="infobox-label" scope="row">Prevention</th><td class="infobox-data">Early exposure to potential allergens<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Sic2014_5-0"><a href="#cite_note-Sic2014-5">[5]</a></sup></td></tr><tr><th class="infobox-label" scope="row">Treatment</th><td class="infobox-data">Avoiding known allergens, medications, <a href="/wiki/Allergen_immunotherapy" title="Allergen immunotherapy">allergen immunotherapy</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NIH2015Imm_6-0"><a href="/wiki/Corticosteroid" title="Corticosteroid">Steroids</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Histamine_antagonist" title="Histamine antagonist">antihistamines</a>, <a href="/wiki/Epinephrine_(medication)" title="Epinephrine (medication)">epinephrine</a>, <a href="/wiki/Mast_cell_stabilizer" title="Mast cell stabilizer">mast cell stabilizers</a>, <a href="/wiki/Antileukotriene" title="Antileukotriene">antileukotrienes</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NIH2015Imm_6-1"><a href="#cite_note-NIH2015Imm-6">[6]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Review09_7-0"><a href="#cite_note-Review09-7">[7]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-8"><a href="#cite_note-8">[8]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-9"><a href="#cite_note-9">[9]</a></sup></td></tr><tr><th class="infobox-label" scope="row">Frequency</th><td class="infobox-data">Common<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NIH2015Epi_10-0"><a href="#cite_note-NIH2015Epi-10">[10]</a></sup></td></tr></tbody></table>
<p><b>Allergies</b>, also known as <b>allergic diseases</b>, are a number of conditions caused by <a href="/wiki/Hypersensitivity" title="Hypersensitivity">hypersensitivity</a> of the <a href="/wiki/Immune_system" title="Immune system">immune system</a> to typically harmless substances in the environment.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Con2007_11-0"><a href="#cite_note-Con2007-11">[11]</a></sup> These diseases include <a href="/wiki/Allergic_rhinitis" title="Allergic rhinitis">hay fever</a>, <a href="/wiki/Food_allergy" title="Food allergy">food allergies</a>, <a href="/wiki/Atopic_dermatitis" title="Atopic dermatitis">atopic dermatitis</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Allergic_asthma" title="Allergic asthma">allergic asthma</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Anaphylaxis" title="Anaphylaxis">anaphylaxis</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NIH2015Types_1-1"><a href="#cite_note-NIH2015Types-1">[1]</a></sup> Symptoms may include <a href="/wiki/Allergic_conjunctivitis" title="Allergic conjunctivitis">red eyes</a>, an itchy <a href="/wiki/Rash" title="Rash">rash</a>, <a href="/wiki/Sneeze" title="Sneeze">sneezing</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Coughing" title="Coughing">coughing</a>, a <a href="/wiki/Rhinorrhea" title="Rhinorrhea">runny nose</a>, <a href="/wiki/Shortness_of_breath" title="Shortness of breath">shortness of breath</a>, or swelling.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NIH2015Sym_12-0"><a href="#cite_note-NIH2015Sym-12">[12]</a></sup> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Food_intolerances" title="Food intolerances">Food intolerances</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Food_poisoning" title="Food poisoning">food poisoning</a> are separate conditions.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NIH2012pdf_3-1"><a href="#cite_note-NIH2012pdf-3">[3]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Bah2012_4-1"><a href="#cite_note-Bah2012-4">[4]</a></sup>
</p><p>Common <a href="/wiki/Allergen" title="Allergen">allergens</a> include <a href="/wiki/Pollen" title="Pollen">pollen</a> and certain foods.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Con2007_11-1"><a href="#cite_note-Con2007-11">[11]</a></sup> Metals and other substances may also cause such problems.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Con2007_11-2"><a href="#cite_note-Con2007-11">[11]</a></sup> Food, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Insect_sting" title="Insect sting">insect stings</a>, and medications are common causes of severe reactions.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Kay2000_2-1"><a href="#cite_note-Kay2000-2">[2]</a></sup> Their development is due to both genetic and environmental factors.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Kay2000_2-2"><a href="#cite_note-Kay2000-2">[2]</a></sup> The underlying mechanism involves <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Immunoglobulin_E_antibodies" title="Immunoglobulin E antibodies">immunoglobulin E antibodies</a> (IgE), part of the body's immune system, binding to an allergen and then to <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Fc%CE%B5RI" title="FcεRI">a receptor</a> on <a href="/wiki/Mast_cell" title="Mast cell">mast cells</a> or <a href="/wiki/Basophil" title="Basophil">basophils</a> where it triggers the release of inflammatory chemicals such as <a href="/wiki/Histamine" title="Histamine">histamine</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-13"><a href="#cite_note-13">[13]</a></sup> Diagnosis is typically based on a person's <a href="/wiki/Medical_history" title="Medical history">medical history</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NIH2012pdf_3-2"><a href="#cite_note-NIH2012pdf-3">[3]</a></sup> Further testing of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Skin_prick_test" title="Skin prick test">skin</a> or blood may be useful in certain cases.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NIH2012pdf_3-3"><a href="#cite_note-NIH2012pdf-3">[3]</a></sup> Positive tests, however, may not necessarily mean there is a significant allergy to the substance in question.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Cox2008_14-0"><a href="#cite_note-Cox2008-14">[14]</a></sup>
</p><p>Early exposure to potential allergens may be protective.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Sic2014_5-1"><a href="#cite_note-Sic2014-5">[5]</a></sup> Treatments for allergies include avoidance of known allergens and the use of medications such as <a href="/wiki/Corticosteroid" title="Corticosteroid">steroids</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Histamine_antagonist" title="Histamine antagonist">antihistamines</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NIH2015Imm_6-2"><a href="#cite_note-NIH2015Imm-6">[6]</a></sup> In severe reactions, injectable <a class="mw-redirect mw-disambig" href="/wiki/Adrenalin" title="Adrenalin">adrenalin</a> (epinephrine) is recommended.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Review09_7-1"><a href="#cite_note-Review09-7">[7]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Allergen_immunotherapy" title="Allergen immunotherapy">Allergen immunotherapy</a>, which gradually exposes people to larger and larger amounts of allergen, is useful for some types of allergies such as hay fever and reactions to insect bites.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NIH2015Imm_6-3"><a href="#cite_note-NIH2015Imm-6">[6]</a></sup> Its use in food allergies is unclear.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NIH2015Imm_6-4"><a href="#cite_note-NIH2015Imm-6">[6]</a></sup>
</p><p>Allergies are common.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NIH2015Epi_10-1"><a href="#cite_note-NIH2015Epi-10">[10]</a></sup> In the developed world, about 20% of people are affected by allergic rhinitis,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NEJM2015_15-0"><a href="#cite_note-NEJM2015-15">[15]</a></sup> about 6% of people have at least one food allergy,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NIH2012pdf_3-4"><a href="#cite_note-NIH2012pdf-3">[3]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Sic2014_5-2"><a href="#cite_note-Sic2014-5">[5]</a></sup> and about 20% have or have had atopic dermatitis at some point in time.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-16"><a href="#cite_note-16">[16]</a></sup> Depending on the country, about 1–18% of people have asthma.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-GINA2015p2_17-0"><a href="#cite_note-GINA2015p2-17">[17]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-GINA2011p2_18-0"><a href="#cite_note-GINA2011p2-18">[18]</a></sup> Anaphylaxis occurs in between 0.05–2% of people.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-19"><a href="#cite_note-19">[19]</a></sup> Rates of many allergic diseases appear to be increasing.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Review09_7-2"><a href="#cite_note-Review09-7">[7]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-20"><a href="#cite_note-20">[20]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-21"><a href="#cite_note-21">[21]</a></sup> The word "allergy" was first used by <a href="/wiki/Clemens_von_Pirquet" title="Clemens von Pirquet">Clemens von Pirquet</a> in 1906.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Kay2000_2-3"><a href="#cite_note-Kay2000-2">[2]</a></sup>
</p>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Signs_and_symptoms">Signs and symptoms</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Allergy&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: Signs and symptoms">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<table class="wikitable" style="width:50%; float:right; font-size:90%; margin-left:15px">
<tbody><tr>
<th>Affected organ</th>
<th>Common signs and symptoms
</th></tr>
<tr>
<td><a href="/wiki/Human_nose" title="Human nose">Nose</a></td>
<td>Swelling of the nasal <a href="/wiki/Mucous_membrane" title="Mucous membrane">mucosa</a> (<a href="/wiki/Rhinitis#Allergic" title="Rhinitis">allergic rhinitis</a>) runny nose, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sneezing" title="Sneezing">sneezing</a>
</td></tr>
<tr>
<td><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Paranasal_sinus" title="Paranasal sinus">Sinuses</a></td>
<td>Allergic <a href="/wiki/Sinusitis" title="Sinusitis">sinusitis</a>
</td></tr>
<tr>
<td><a href="/wiki/Human_eye" title="Human eye">Eyes</a></td>
<td>Redness and <a href="/wiki/Itch" title="Itch">itching</a> of the <a href="/wiki/Conjunctiva" title="Conjunctiva">conjunctiva</a> (allergic conjunctivitis, watery)
</td></tr>
<tr>
<td><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Airway" title="Airway">Airways</a></td>
<td>Sneezing, coughing, <a href="/wiki/Bronchoconstriction" title="Bronchoconstriction">bronchoconstriction</a>, <a href="/wiki/Wheeze" title="Wheeze">wheezing</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Dyspnea" title="Dyspnea">dyspnea</a>, sometimes outright attacks of <a href="/wiki/Asthma" title="Asthma">asthma</a>, in severe cases the airway constricts due to swelling known as <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Laryngeal_edema" title="Laryngeal edema">laryngeal edema</a>
</td></tr>
<tr>
<td><a href="/wiki/Ear" title="Ear">Ears</a></td>
<td>Feeling of fullness, possibly pain, and impaired hearing due to the lack of <a href="/wiki/Eustachian_tube" title="Eustachian tube">eustachian tube</a> drainage.
</td></tr>
<tr>
<td><a href="/wiki/Skin" title="Skin">Skin</a></td>
<td><a href="/wiki/Rash" title="Rash">Rashes</a>, such as <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Eczema" title="Eczema">eczema</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Urticaria" title="Urticaria">hives (urticaria)</a>
</td></tr>
<tr>
<td><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Human_gastrointestinal_tract" title="Human gastrointestinal tract">Gastrointestinal tract</a></td>
<td><a href="/wiki/Abdominal_pain" title="Abdominal pain">Abdominal pain</a>, <a href="/wiki/Bloating" title="Bloating">bloating</a>, <a href="/wiki/Vomiting" title="Vomiting">vomiting</a>, <a href="/wiki/Diarrhea" title="Diarrhea">diarrhea</a>
</td></tr></tbody></table>
<p>Many allergens such as dust or pollen are airborne particles. In these cases, symptoms arise in areas in contact with air, such as the eyes, nose, and lungs. For instance, allergic rhinitis, also known as hay fever, causes irritation of the nose, sneezing, itching, and redness of the eyes.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Conn's_Current_Therapy_2005_22-0"><a href="#cite_note-Conn's_Current_Therapy_2005-22">[22]</a></sup> Inhaled allergens can also lead to increased production of <a href="/wiki/Mucus" title="Mucus">mucus</a> in the <a href="/wiki/Lung" title="Lung">lungs</a>, <a href="/wiki/Shortness_of_breath" title="Shortness of breath">shortness of breath</a>, coughing, and wheezing.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-holgate98_23-0"><a href="#cite_note-holgate98-23">[23]</a></sup>
</p><p>Aside from these ambient allergens, allergic reactions can result from <a href="/wiki/Food" title="Food">foods</a>, <a href="/wiki/Insect_sting_allergy" title="Insect sting allergy">insect stings</a>, and reactions to <a href="/wiki/Medication" title="Medication">medications</a> like <a href="/wiki/Aspirin" title="Aspirin">aspirin</a> and <a href="/wiki/Antibiotic" title="Antibiotic">antibiotics</a> such as <a href="/wiki/Penicillin" title="Penicillin">penicillin</a>. Symptoms of food allergy include abdominal pain, <a href="/wiki/Bloating" title="Bloating">bloating</a>, vomiting, <a href="/wiki/Diarrhea" title="Diarrhea">diarrhea</a>, <a href="/wiki/Itch" title="Itch">itchy</a> skin, and <a href="/wiki/Angioedema" title="Angioedema">hives</a>. Food allergies rarely cause <a href="/wiki/Respiratory_tract" title="Respiratory tract">respiratory</a> (asthmatic) reactions, or <a href="/wiki/Rhinitis" title="Rhinitis">rhinitis</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-rusznak98_24-0"><a href="#cite_note-rusznak98-24">[24]</a></sup> Insect stings, food, <a href="/wiki/Antibiotic" title="Antibiotic">antibiotics</a>, and certain medicines may produce a systemic allergic response that is also called anaphylaxis; multiple organ systems can be affected, including the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Digestive_system" title="Digestive system">digestive system</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Respiratory_system" title="Respiratory system">respiratory system</a>, and the <a href="/wiki/Circulatory_system" title="Circulatory system">circulatory system</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Insect_sting_anaphylaxis_25-0"><a href="#cite_note-Insect_sting_anaphylaxis-25">[25]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Penicillin_allergy_skin_testing:_what_do_we_do_now?_26-0"><a href="#cite_note-Penicillin_allergy_skin_testing:_what_do_we_do_now?-26">[26]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-tang03_27-0"><a href="#cite_note-tang03-27">[27]</a></sup> Depending on the severity, anaphylaxis can include skin reactions, bronchoconstriction, <a href="/wiki/Edema" title="Edema">swelling</a>, <a href="/wiki/Hypotension" title="Hypotension">low blood pressure</a>, <a href="/wiki/Coma" title="Coma">coma</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Death" title="Death">death</a>. This type of reaction can be triggered suddenly, or the onset can be delayed. The nature of anaphylaxis is such that the reaction can seem to be subsiding but may recur throughout a period of time.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-tang03_27-1"><a href="#cite_note-tang03-27">[27]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Skin">Skin</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Allergy&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: Skin">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Substances that come into contact with the skin, such as <a href="/wiki/Latex" title="Latex">latex</a>, are also common causes of allergic reactions, known as <a href="/wiki/Contact_dermatitis" title="Contact dermatitis">contact dermatitis</a> or eczema.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Natural_rubber_latex_allergy:_a_problem_of_interdisciplinary_concern_in_medicine_28-0"><a href="#cite_note-Natural_rubber_latex_allergy:_a_problem_of_interdisciplinary_concern_in_medicine-28">[28]</a></sup> Skin allergies frequently cause rashes, or swelling and inflammation within the skin, in what is known as a "<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Wheal_response" title="Wheal response">weal</a> and flare" reaction characteristic of hives and <a href="/wiki/Angioedema" title="Angioedema">angioedema</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Urticaria_and_angioedema:_a_practical_approach_29-0"><a href="#cite_note-Urticaria_and_angioedema:_a_practical_approach-29">[29]</a></sup>
</p><p>With insect stings, a large local reaction may occur in the form of an area of skin redness greater than 10 cm in size that can last one to two days.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Lud2015_30-0"><a href="#cite_note-Lud2015-30">[30]</a></sup> This reaction may also occur after <a href="/wiki/Immunotherapy" title="Immunotherapy">immunotherapy</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-31"><a href="#cite_note-31">[31]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Cause">Cause</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Allergy&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: Cause">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>Risk factors for allergies can be placed in two broad categories, namely <a href="/wiki/Host_(biology)" title="Host (biology)">host</a> and <a href="/wiki/Natural_environment" title="Natural environment">environmental</a> factors.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-The_genetic_and_environmental_basis_of_atopic_diseases_32-0"><a href="#cite_note-The_genetic_and_environmental_basis_of_atopic_diseases-32">[32]</a></sup> Host factors include <a href="/wiki/Heredity" title="Heredity">heredity</a>, <a href="/wiki/Sex" title="Sex">sex</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Race_(classification_of_human_beings)" title="Race (classification of human beings)">race</a>, and age, with heredity being by far the most significant. However, there have been recent increases in the incidence of allergic disorders that cannot be explained by genetic factors alone. Four major environmental candidates are alterations in exposure to <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Infectious_disease" title="Infectious disease">infectious diseases</a> during early childhood, environmental <a href="/wiki/Pollution" title="Pollution">pollution</a>, allergen levels, and <a href="/wiki/Diet_(nutrition)" title="Diet (nutrition)">dietary</a> changes.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Janeway_33-0"><a href="#cite_note-Janeway-33">[33]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Dust_mites">Dust mites</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Allergy&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: Dust mites">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Dust_mite_allergy" title="Dust mite allergy">Dust mite allergy</a></div>
<p>Dust mite allergy, also known as house dust allergy, is a <a href="/wiki/Sensitization_(immunology)" title="Sensitization (immunology)">sensitization</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Allergic_reaction" title="Allergic reaction">allergic reaction</a> to the droppings of <a href="/wiki/House_dust_mite" title="House dust mite">house dust mites</a>. The allergy is common<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-34"><a href="#cite_note-34">[34]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-35"><a href="#cite_note-35">[35]</a></sup> and can trigger allergic reactions such as asthma, <a href="/wiki/Dermatitis" title="Dermatitis">eczema</a>, or <a href="/wiki/Itch" title="Itch">itching</a>. It is the manifestation of a <a href="/wiki/Parasitic_disease" title="Parasitic disease">parasitosis</a>. The mite's gut contains potent digestive enzymes (notably <a href="/wiki/Peptidase_1_(mite)" title="Peptidase 1 (mite)">peptidase 1</a>) that persist in their feces and are major inducers of allergic reactions such as <a href="/wiki/Wheeze" title="Wheeze">wheezing</a>. The mite's exoskeleton can also contribute to allergic reactions. Unlike <a href="/wiki/Scabies" title="Scabies">scabies</a> mites or skin follicle mites, house dust mites do not burrow under the skin and are not parasitic.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-unl_36-0"><a href="#cite_note-unl-36">[36]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Foods">Foods</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Allergy&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: Foods">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Food_allergy" title="Food allergy">Food allergy</a></div>
<p>A wide variety of foods can cause allergic reactions, but 90% of allergic responses to foods are caused by cow's <a href="/wiki/Milk" title="Milk">milk</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Soy" title="Soy">soy</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Egg_(food)" title="Egg (food)">eggs</a>, <a href="/wiki/Wheat" title="Wheat">wheat</a>, <a href="/wiki/Peanut" title="Peanut">peanuts</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Tree_nuts" title="Tree nuts">tree nuts</a>, <a href="/wiki/Fish" title="Fish">fish</a>, and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Crustacea" title="Crustacea">shellfish</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-aafa.org_37-0"><a href="#cite_note-aafa.org-37">[37]</a></sup> Other <a href="/wiki/Food_allergy" title="Food allergy">food allergies</a>, affecting less than 1 person per 10,000 population, may be considered "rare".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Maleki_38-0"><a href="#cite_note-Maleki-38">[38]</a></sup> The use of hydrolyzed milk <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Baby_formula" title="Baby formula">baby formula</a> versus standard milk baby formula does not appear to affect the risk.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-39"><a href="#cite_note-39">[39]</a></sup>
</p><p>The most common food allergy in the US population is a sensitivity to <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Crustacea" title="Crustacea">crustacea</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Maleki_38-1"><a href="#cite_note-Maleki-38">[38]</a></sup> Although peanut allergies are notorious for their severity, peanut allergies are not the most common food allergy in adults or children. Severe or life-threatening reactions may be triggered by other allergens and are more common when combined with asthma.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-aafa.org_37-1"><a href="#cite_note-aafa.org-37">[37]</a></sup>
</p><p>Rates of allergies differ between adults and children. Children can sometimes outgrow <a href="/wiki/Peanut" title="Peanut">peanut</a> allergies. Egg allergies affect one to two percent of children but are outgrown by about two-thirds of children by the age of 5.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-40"><a href="#cite_note-40">[40]</a></sup> The sensitivity is usually to proteins in the white, rather than the <a href="/wiki/Yolk" title="Yolk">yolk</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Sicherer_63_41-0"><a href="#cite_note-Sicherer_63-41">[41]</a></sup>
</p><p>Milk-protein allergies are most common in children.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-42"><a href="#cite_note-42">[42]</a></sup> Approximately 60% of milk-protein reactions are <a href="/wiki/Immunoglobulin_E" title="Immunoglobulin E">immunoglobulin E</a>-mediated, with the remaining usually attributable to <a href="/wiki/Proctocolitis" title="Proctocolitis">inflammation of the colon</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-43"><a href="#cite_note-43">[43]</a></sup> Some people are unable to tolerate milk from goats or sheep as well as from cows, and many are also unable to tolerate <a href="/wiki/Dairy" title="Dairy">dairy</a> products such as cheese. Roughly 10% of children with a milk allergy will have a reaction to <a href="/wiki/Beef" title="Beef">beef</a>. Beef contains small amounts of proteins that are present in greater abundance in cow's milk.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-44"><a href="#cite_note-44">[44]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Lactose_intolerance" title="Lactose intolerance">Lactose intolerance</a>, a common reaction to milk, is not a form of allergy at all, but due to the absence of an <a href="/wiki/Enzyme" title="Enzyme">enzyme</a> in the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Digestive_tract" title="Digestive tract">digestive tract</a>.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (July 2022)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup>
</p><p>Those with <a href="/wiki/Nut_(fruit)" title="Nut (fruit)">tree nut</a> allergies may be allergic to one or to many tree nuts, including <a href="/wiki/Pecan" title="Pecan">pecans</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Pistachios" title="Pistachios">pistachios</a>, <a href="/wiki/Pine_nut" title="Pine nut">pine nuts</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Walnut" title="Walnut">walnuts</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Sicherer_63_41-1"><a href="#cite_note-Sicherer_63-41">[41]</a></sup> In addition, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Seeds" title="Seeds">seeds</a>, including <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sesame_seeds" title="Sesame seeds">sesame seeds</a> and <a href="/wiki/Poppy_seed" title="Poppy seed">poppy seeds</a>, contain oils in which protein is present, which may elicit an allergic reaction.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Sicherer_63_41-2"><a href="#cite_note-Sicherer_63-41">[41]</a></sup>
</p><p>Allergens can be transferred from one food to another through <a href="/wiki/Genetic_engineering" title="Genetic engineering">genetic engineering</a>; however genetic modification can also remove allergens. Little research has been done on the natural variation of allergen concentrations in unmodified crops.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-45"><a href="#cite_note-45">[45]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-46"><a href="#cite_note-46">[46]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Latex">Latex</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Allergy&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: Latex">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p><a href="/wiki/Latex" title="Latex">Latex</a> can trigger an IgE-mediated cutaneous, respiratory, and systemic reaction. The prevalence of latex allergy in the general population is believed to be less than one percent. In a hospital study, 1 in 800 surgical patients (0.125 percent) reported latex sensitivity, although the sensitivity among healthcare workers is higher, between seven and ten percent. Researchers attribute this higher level to the exposure of healthcare workers to areas with significant airborne latex allergens, such as operating rooms, intensive-care units, and dental suites. These latex-rich environments may sensitize healthcare workers who regularly inhale allergenic proteins.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Sussman_47-0"><a href="#cite_note-Sussman-47">[47]</a></sup>
</p><p>The most prevalent response to latex is an allergic contact dermatitis, a delayed hypersensitive reaction appearing as dry, crusted lesions. This reaction usually lasts 48–96 hours. Sweating or rubbing the area under the glove aggravates the lesions, possibly leading to ulcerations.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Sussman_47-1"><a href="#cite_note-Sussman-47">[47]</a></sup> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Anaphylactic" title="Anaphylactic">Anaphylactic</a> reactions occur most often in sensitive patients who have been exposed to a surgeon's latex gloves during abdominal surgery, but other <a href="/wiki/Mucous_membrane" title="Mucous membrane">mucosal</a> exposures, such as dental procedures, can also produce systemic reactions.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Sussman_47-2"><a href="#cite_note-Sussman-47">[47]</a></sup>
</p><p>Latex and <a href="/wiki/Banana" title="Banana">banana</a> sensitivity may cross-react.  Furthermore, those with latex allergy may also have sensitivities to <a href="/wiki/Avocado" title="Avocado">avocado</a>, kiwifruit, and chestnut.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-48"><a href="#cite_note-48">[48]</a></sup> These people often have <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Perioral" title="Perioral">perioral</a> itching and local <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Urticaria" title="Urticaria">urticaria</a>. Only occasionally have these food-induced allergies induced systemic responses. Researchers suspect that the cross-reactivity of latex with banana, <a href="/wiki/Avocado" title="Avocado">avocado</a>, <a href="/wiki/Kiwifruit" title="Kiwifruit">kiwifruit</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Chestnut" title="Chestnut">chestnut</a> occurs because latex proteins are structurally <a href="/wiki/Homology_(biology)" title="Homology (biology)">homologous</a> with some other plant proteins.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Sussman_47-3"><a href="#cite_note-Sussman-47">[47]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Medications">Medications</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Allergy&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: Medications">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Drug_allergy" title="Drug allergy">Drug allergy</a></div>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/Adverse_drug_reaction" title="Adverse drug reaction">Adverse drug reaction</a> and <a href="/wiki/Drug_eruption" title="Drug eruption">Drug eruption</a></div>
<p>About 10% of people report that they are allergic to <a href="/wiki/Penicillin" title="Penicillin">penicillin</a>; however, 90% turn out not to be.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Al2015_49-0"><a href="#cite_note-Al2015-49">[49]</a></sup> Serious allergies only occur in about 0.03%.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Al2015_49-1"><a href="#cite_note-Al2015-49">[49]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Insect_stings">Insect stings</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Allergy&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: Insect stings">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Insect_sting_allergy" title="Insect sting allergy">Insect sting allergy</a></div>
<p>Typically, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Insects" title="Insects">insects</a> which generate allergic responses are either stinging insects (<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Wasps" title="Wasps">wasps</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Bees" title="Bees">bees</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hornets" title="Hornets">hornets</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ants" title="Ants">ants</a>) or biting insects (<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Mosquitoes" title="Mosquitoes">mosquitoes</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ticks" title="Ticks">ticks</a>). Stinging insects inject venom into their victims, whilst biting insects normally introduce <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Anti-coagulants" title="Anti-coagulants">anti-coagulants</a>.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (July 2022)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Toxins_interacting_with_proteins">Toxins interacting with proteins</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Allergy&amp;action=edit&amp;section=9" title="Edit section: Toxins interacting with proteins">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Another non-food protein reaction, <a href="/wiki/Urushiol-induced_contact_dermatitis" title="Urushiol-induced contact dermatitis">urushiol-induced contact dermatitis</a>, originates after contact with <a href="/wiki/Poison_ivy" title="Poison ivy">poison ivy</a>, <a href="/wiki/Toxicodendron_pubescens" title="Toxicodendron pubescens">eastern poison oak</a>, <a href="/wiki/Toxicodendron_diversilobum" title="Toxicodendron diversilobum">western poison oak</a>, or <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Poison_sumac" title="Poison sumac">poison sumac</a>. <a href="/wiki/Urushiol" title="Urushiol">Urushiol</a>, which is not itself a protein, acts as a <a href="/wiki/Hapten" title="Hapten">hapten</a> and chemically reacts with, binds to, and changes the shape of <a href="/wiki/Integral_membrane_protein" title="Integral membrane protein">integral membrane proteins</a> on exposed skin cells. The immune system does not recognize the affected cells as normal parts of the body, causing a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/T-cell" title="T-cell">T-cell</a>-mediated <a href="/wiki/Immune_response" title="Immune response">immune response</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-50"><a href="#cite_note-50">[50]</a></sup> Of these poisonous plants, sumac is the most virulent.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-51"><a href="#cite_note-51">[51]</a></sup> The resulting dermatological response to the reaction between urushiol and membrane proteins includes redness, swelling, <a href="/wiki/Papule" title="Papule">papules</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Vesicle_(dermatology)" title="Vesicle (dermatology)">vesicles</a>, <a href="/wiki/Blister" title="Blister">blisters</a>, and streaking.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Dermatlas_52-0"><a href="#cite_note-Dermatlas-52">[52]</a></sup>
</p><p>Estimates vary on the percentage of the population that will have an immune system response. Approximately 25 percent of the population will have a strong allergic response to urushiol. In general, approximately 80 percent to 90 percent of adults will develop a rash if they are exposed to .0050 milligrams (7.7<span style="margin:0 .15em 0 .25em">×</span>10<sup>−5</sup> gr) of purified urushiol, but some people are so sensitive that it takes only a molecular trace on the skin to initiate an allergic reaction.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-53"><a href="#cite_note-53">[53]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Genetics">Genetics</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Allergy&amp;action=edit&amp;section=10" title="Edit section: Genetics">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Allergic diseases are strongly <a href="/wiki/Family" title="Family">familial</a>: <a href="/wiki/Twin#Monozygotic_twins" title="Twin">identical twins</a> are likely to have the same allergic diseases about 70% of the time; the same allergy occurs about 40% of the time in <a href="/wiki/Twin#Dizygotic_twins" title="Twin">non-identical twins</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Allergy_54-0"><a href="#cite_note-Allergy-54">[54]</a></sup> Allergic parents are more likely to have allergic children,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-DeSwert_55-0"><a href="#cite_note-DeSwert-55">[55]</a></sup> and those children's allergies are likely to be more severe than those in children of non-allergic parents. Some allergies, however, are not consistent along <a href="/wiki/Genealogy" title="Genealogy">genealogies</a>; parents who are allergic to <a href="/wiki/Peanut" title="Peanut">peanuts</a> may have children who are allergic to <a href="/wiki/Ragweed" title="Ragweed">ragweed</a>. The likelihood of developing allergies is <a href="/wiki/Heredity" title="Heredity">inherited</a> and related to an irregularity in the immune system, but the specific <a href="/wiki/Allergen" title="Allergen">allergen</a> is not.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-DeSwert_55-1"><a href="#cite_note-DeSwert-55">[55]</a></sup>
</p><p>The risk of allergic <a href="/wiki/Sensitization_(immunology)" title="Sensitization (immunology)">sensitization</a> and the development of allergies varies with age, with young children most at risk.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Croner_56-0"><a href="#cite_note-Croner-56">[56]</a></sup> Several studies have shown that IgE levels are highest in childhood and fall rapidly between the ages of 10 and 30 years.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Croner_56-1"><a href="#cite_note-Croner-56">[56]</a></sup> The peak prevalence of hay fever is highest in children and young adults and the incidence of asthma is highest in children under 10.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-57"><a href="#cite_note-57">[57]</a></sup>
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Ethnic_group" title="Ethnic group">Ethnicity</a> may play a role in some allergies; however, racial factors have been difficult to separate from environmental influences and changes due to <a href="/wiki/Human_migration" title="Human migration">migration</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-DeSwert_55-2"><a href="#cite_note-DeSwert-55">[55]</a></sup> It has been suggested that different <a href="/wiki/Locus_(genetics)" title="Locus (genetics)">genetic loci</a> are responsible for asthma, to be specific, in people of <a href="/wiki/Caucasian_race" title="Caucasian race">European</a>, <a href="/wiki/Hispanic" title="Hispanic">Hispanic</a>, <a href="/wiki/Asian_people" title="Asian people">Asian</a>, and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ethnic_groups_of_Africa" title="Ethnic groups of Africa">African</a> origins.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-African_Americans_with_asthma:_genetic_insights_58-0"><a href="#cite_note-African_Americans_with_asthma:_genetic_insights-58">[58]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Hygiene_hypothesis">Hygiene hypothesis</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Allergy&amp;action=edit&amp;section=11" title="Edit section: Hygiene hypothesis">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Hygiene_hypothesis" title="Hygiene hypothesis">Hygiene hypothesis</a></div>
<p>Allergic diseases are caused by inappropriate immunological responses to harmless <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Antigens" title="Antigens">antigens</a> driven by a <a href="/wiki/T_helper_cell" title="T helper cell">TH2</a>-mediated immune response. Many <a href="/wiki/Bacteria" title="Bacteria">bacteria</a> and <a href="/wiki/Virus" title="Virus">viruses</a> elicit a <a href="/wiki/T_helper_cell" title="T helper cell">TH1</a>-mediated immune response, which down-regulates TH2 responses. The first proposed mechanism of action of the hygiene hypothesis was that insufficient stimulation of the TH1 arm of the immune system leads to an overactive TH2 arm, which in turn leads to allergic disease.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-59"><a href="#cite_note-59">[59]</a></sup> In other words, individuals living in too sterile an environment are not exposed to enough pathogens to keep the immune system busy. Since our bodies evolved to deal with a certain level of such pathogens, when they are not exposed to this level, the immune system will attack harmless antigens, and thus normally benign microbial objects—like pollen—will trigger an immune response.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-60"><a href="#cite_note-60">[60]</a></sup>
</p><p>The hygiene hypothesis was developed to explain the observation that <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hay_fever" title="Hay fever">hay fever</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Eczema" title="Eczema">eczema</a>, both allergic diseases, were less common in children from larger families, which were, it is presumed, exposed to more infectious agents through their siblings, than in children from families with only one child. The hygiene hypothesis has been extensively investigated by <a href="/wiki/Immunology" title="Immunology">immunologists</a> and <a href="/wiki/Epidemiology" title="Epidemiology">epidemiologists</a> and has become an important theoretical framework for the study of allergic disorders. It is used to explain the increase in allergic diseases that have been seen since <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Industrialization" title="Industrialization">industrialization</a>, and the higher incidence of allergic diseases in more developed countries. The hygiene hypothesis has now expanded to include exposure to symbiotic bacteria and parasites as important modulators of immune system development, along with infectious agents.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (July 2022)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup>
</p><p>Epidemiological data support the hygiene hypothesis. Studies have shown that various immunological and autoimmune diseases are much less common in the developing world than the industrialized world, and that immigrants to the industrialized world from the developing world increasingly develop immunological disorders in relation to the length of time since arrival in the industrialized world.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pmid12910582_61-0"><a href="#cite_note-pmid12910582-61">[61]</a></sup> Longitudinal studies in the third world demonstrate an increase in immunological disorders as a country grows more affluent and, it is presumed, cleaner.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pmid17326711_62-0"><a href="#cite_note-pmid17326711-62">[62]</a></sup> The use of antibiotics in the first year of life has been linked to asthma and other allergic diseases.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Does_antibiotic_exposure_during_infancy_lead_to_development_of_asthma?:_a_systematic_review_and_metaanalysis_63-0"><a href="#cite_note-Does_antibiotic_exposure_during_infancy_lead_to_development_of_asthma?:_a_systematic_review_and_metaanalysis-63">[63]</a></sup> The use of antibacterial cleaning products has also been associated with higher incidence of asthma, as has birth by <a href="/wiki/Caesarean_section" title="Caesarean section">Caesarean section</a> rather than vaginal birth.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-A_meta-analysis_of_the_association_between_Caesarean_section_and_childhood_asthma_64-0"><a href="#cite_note-A_meta-analysis_of_the_association_between_Caesarean_section_and_childhood_asthma-64">[64]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-The_use_of_household_cleaning_sprays_and_adult_asthma:_an_international_longitudinal_study_65-0"><a href="#cite_note-The_use_of_household_cleaning_sprays_and_adult_asthma:_an_international_longitudinal_study-65">[65]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Stress">Stress</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Allergy&amp;action=edit&amp;section=12" title="Edit section: Stress">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Chronic <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Stress_(psychological)" title="Stress (psychological)">stress</a> can aggravate allergic conditions. This has been attributed to a T helper 2 (TH2)-predominant response driven by suppression of <a href="/wiki/Interleukin_12" title="Interleukin 12">interleukin 12</a> by both the <a href="/wiki/Autonomic_nervous_system" title="Autonomic nervous system">autonomic nervous system</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Hypothalamic%E2%80%93pituitary%E2%80%93adrenal_axis" title="Hypothalamic–pituitary–adrenal axis">hypothalamic–pituitary–adrenal axis</a>. Stress management in highly susceptible individuals may improve symptoms.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-66"><a href="#cite_note-66">[66]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Other_environmental_factors">Other environmental factors</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Allergy&amp;action=edit&amp;section=13" title="Edit section: Other environmental factors">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Allergic diseases are more common in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Industrialized" title="Industrialized">industrialized</a> countries than in countries that are more traditional or <a href="/wiki/Agriculture" title="Agriculture">agricultural</a>, and there is a higher rate of allergic disease in <a href="/wiki/Urban_area" title="Urban area">urban</a> populations versus <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Rural" title="Rural">rural</a> populations, although these differences are becoming less defined.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-cooper04_67-0"><a href="#cite_note-cooper04-67">[67]</a></sup> Historically, the trees planted in urban areas were predominantly male to prevent litter from seeds and fruits, but the high ratio of male trees causes high pollen counts, a phenomenon that horticulturist Tom Ogren has called "<a href="/wiki/Botanical_sexism" title="Botanical sexism">botanical sexism</a>".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-68"><a href="#cite_note-68">[68]</a></sup>
</p><p>Alterations in exposure to <a href="/wiki/Microorganism" title="Microorganism">microorganisms</a> is another plausible explanation, at present, for the increase in <a href="/wiki/Atopy" title="Atopy">atopic allergy</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Janeway_33-1"><a href="#cite_note-Janeway-33">[33]</a></sup> Endotoxin exposure reduces release of inflammatory <a href="/wiki/Cytokine" title="Cytokine">cytokines</a> such as <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Tumor_necrosis_factor_alpha" title="Tumor necrosis factor alpha">TNF-α</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Interferon-gamma" title="Interferon-gamma">IFNγ</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Interleukin-10" title="Interleukin-10">interleukin-10</a>, and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Interleukin-12" title="Interleukin-12">interleukin-12</a> from white blood cells (<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Leukocytes" title="Leukocytes">leukocytes</a>) that circulate in the <a href="/wiki/Blood" title="Blood">blood</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pmid12239255_69-0"><a href="#cite_note-pmid12239255-69">[69]</a></sup> Certain microbe-sensing <a href="/wiki/Protein" title="Protein">proteins</a>, known as <a href="/wiki/Toll-like_receptor" title="Toll-like receptor">Toll-like receptors</a>, found on the surface of cells in the body are also thought to be involved in these processes.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Epidemiological_and_immunological_evidence_for_the_hygiene_hypothesis_70-0"><a href="#cite_note-Epidemiological_and_immunological_evidence_for_the_hygiene_hypothesis-70">[70]</a></sup>
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Gutworm" title="Gutworm">Gutworms</a> and similar parasites are present in untreated drinking water in developing countries, and were present in the water of developed countries until the routine <a href="/wiki/Water_chlorination" title="Water chlorination">chlorination</a> and purification of drinking water supplies.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Parasitic_food-borne_and_water-borne_zoonoses_71-0"><a href="#cite_note-Parasitic_food-borne_and_water-borne_zoonoses-71">[71]</a></sup> Recent research has shown that some common <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Parasite" title="Parasite">parasites</a>, such as <a href="/wiki/Parasitic_worm" title="Parasitic worm">intestinal worms</a> (e.g., <a href="/wiki/Hookworm" title="Hookworm">hookworms</a>), secrete chemicals into the gut wall (and, hence, the bloodstream) that <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Immunosuppressant" title="Immunosuppressant">suppress</a> the immune system and prevent the body from attacking the parasite.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Worms_and_allergy_72-0"><a href="#cite_note-Worms_and_allergy-72">[72]</a></sup> This gives rise to a new slant on the hygiene hypothesis theory—that <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Co-evolution" title="Co-evolution">co-evolution</a> of humans and parasites has led to an immune system that functions correctly only in the presence of the parasites. Without them, the immune system becomes unbalanced and oversensitive.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Yazdanbakhsh02_73-0"><a href="#cite_note-Yazdanbakhsh02-73">[73]</a></sup> In particular, research suggests that allergies may coincide with the delayed establishment of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Gut_flora" title="Gut flora">gut flora</a> in <a href="/wiki/Infant" title="Infant">infants</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pmid17382394_74-0"><a href="#cite_note-pmid17382394-74">[74]</a></sup> However, the research to support this theory is conflicting, with some studies performed in China and <a href="/wiki/Ethiopia" title="Ethiopia">Ethiopia</a> showing an increase in allergy in people infected with intestinal worms.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-cooper04_67-1"><a href="#cite_note-cooper04-67">[67]</a></sup> Clinical trials have been initiated to test the effectiveness of certain worms in treating some allergies.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-falcone05_75-0"><a href="#cite_note-falcone05-75">[75]</a></sup> It may be that the term 'parasite' could turn out to be inappropriate, and in fact a hitherto unsuspected <a href="/wiki/Mutualism_(biology)" title="Mutualism (biology)">symbiosis</a> is at work.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-falcone05_75-1"><a href="#cite_note-falcone05-75">[75]</a></sup> For more information on this topic, see <a href="/wiki/Helminthic_therapy" title="Helminthic therapy">Helminthic therapy</a>.
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Pathophysiology">Pathophysiology</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Allergy&amp;action=edit&amp;section=14" title="Edit section: Pathophysiology">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:The_Allergy_Pathway.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1348" data-file-width="2122" decoding="async" height="140" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e7/The_Allergy_Pathway.jpg/220px-The_Allergy_Pathway.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e7/The_Allergy_Pathway.jpg/330px-The_Allergy_Pathway.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e7/The_Allergy_Pathway.jpg/440px-The_Allergy_Pathway.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:The_Allergy_Pathway.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A summary diagram that explains how allergy develops</div></div></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Tissues_Affected_In_Allergic_Inflammation.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1596" data-file-width="2037" decoding="async" height="172" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a0/Tissues_Affected_In_Allergic_Inflammation.jpg/220px-Tissues_Affected_In_Allergic_Inflammation.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a0/Tissues_Affected_In_Allergic_Inflammation.jpg/330px-Tissues_Affected_In_Allergic_Inflammation.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a0/Tissues_Affected_In_Allergic_Inflammation.jpg/440px-Tissues_Affected_In_Allergic_Inflammation.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Tissues_Affected_In_Allergic_Inflammation.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Tissues affected in <a href="/wiki/Allergic_inflammation" title="Allergic inflammation">allergic inflammation</a></div></div></div>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Acute_response">Acute response</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Allergy&amp;action=edit&amp;section=15" title="Edit section: Acute response">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Allergy_degranulation_processes_01.svg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1200" data-file-width="1600" decoding="async" height="165" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/36/Allergy_degranulation_processes_01.svg/220px-Allergy_degranulation_processes_01.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/36/Allergy_degranulation_processes_01.svg/330px-Allergy_degranulation_processes_01.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/36/Allergy_degranulation_processes_01.svg/440px-Allergy_degranulation_processes_01.svg.png 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Allergy_degranulation_processes_01.svg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Degranulation process in allergy. Second exposure to allergen. <b>1</b> – antigen; <b>2</b> – IgE antibody; <b>3</b> – FcεRI receptor; <b>4</b> – preformed mediators (histamine, proteases, chemokines, heparin); <b>5</b> – <a href="/wiki/Granule_(cell_biology)" title="Granule (cell biology)">granules</a>; <b>6</b> – <a href="/wiki/Mast_cell" title="Mast cell">mast cell</a>; <b>7</b> – newly formed mediators (prostaglandins, leukotrienes, thromboxanes, <a href="/wiki/Platelet-activating_factor" title="Platelet-activating factor">PAF</a>).</div></div></div>
<p>In the initial stages of allergy, a type I hypersensitivity reaction against an allergen encountered for the first time and presented by a professional <a href="/wiki/Antigen-presenting_cell" title="Antigen-presenting cell">antigen-presenting cell</a> causes a response in a type of immune cell called a <a href="/wiki/T_helper_cell" title="T helper cell">T<sub>H</sub>2 lymphocyte</a>, a subset of <a href="/wiki/T_cell" title="T cell">T cells</a> that produce a <a href="/wiki/Cytokine" title="Cytokine">cytokine</a> called <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Interleukin-4" title="Interleukin-4">interleukin-4</a> (IL-4). These T<sub>H</sub>2 cells interact with other <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Lymphocytes" title="Lymphocytes">lymphocytes</a> called <a href="/wiki/B_cell" title="B cell">B cells</a>, whose role is production of antibodies. Coupled with signals provided by IL-4, this interaction stimulates the B cell to begin production of a large amount of a particular type of antibody known as IgE. Secreted IgE circulates in the blood and binds to an IgE-specific receptor (a kind of <a href="/wiki/Fc_receptor" title="Fc receptor">Fc receptor</a> called <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Fc%CE%B5RI" title="FcεRI">FcεRI</a>) on the surface of other kinds of immune cells called <a href="/wiki/Mast_cell" title="Mast cell">mast cells</a> and <a href="/wiki/Basophil" title="Basophil">basophils</a>, which are both involved in the acute inflammatory response. The IgE-coated cells, at this stage, are sensitized to the allergen.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Janeway_33-2"><a href="#cite_note-Janeway-33">[33]</a></sup>
</p><p>If later exposure to the same allergen occurs, the allergen can bind to the IgE molecules held on the surface of the mast cells or basophils. Cross-linking of the IgE and Fc receptors occurs when more than one IgE-receptor complex interacts with the same allergenic molecule and activates the sensitized cell. Activated mast cells and basophils undergo a process called <a href="/wiki/Degranulation" title="Degranulation">degranulation</a>, during which they release <a href="/wiki/Histamine" title="Histamine">histamine</a> and other inflammatory chemical mediators (<a href="/wiki/Cytokine" title="Cytokine">cytokines</a>, <a href="/wiki/Interleukin" title="Interleukin">interleukins</a>, <a href="/wiki/Leukotriene" title="Leukotriene">leukotrienes</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Prostaglandin" title="Prostaglandin">prostaglandins</a>) from their <a href="/wiki/Granule_(cell_biology)" title="Granule (cell biology)">granules</a> into the surrounding tissue causing several systemic effects, such as <a href="/wiki/Vasodilation" title="Vasodilation">vasodilation</a>, <a href="/wiki/Mucus" title="Mucus">mucous</a> secretion, <a href="/wiki/Nerve" title="Nerve">nerve</a> stimulation, and <a href="/wiki/Smooth_muscle" title="Smooth muscle">smooth muscle</a> contraction. This results in <a href="/wiki/Rhinorrhea" title="Rhinorrhea">rhinorrhea</a>, itchiness, dyspnea, and anaphylaxis. Depending on the individual, allergen, and mode of introduction, the symptoms can be system-wide (classical anaphylaxis) or localized to specific body systems. Asthma is localized to the respiratory system and eczema is localized to the <a href="/wiki/Dermis" title="Dermis">dermis</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Janeway_33-3"><a href="#cite_note-Janeway-33">[33]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Late-phase_response">Late-phase response</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Allergy&amp;action=edit&amp;section=16" title="Edit section: Late-phase response">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>After the chemical mediators of the acute response subside, late-phase responses can often occur. This is due to the migration of other <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Leukocyte" title="Leukocyte">leukocytes</a> such as <a href="/wiki/Neutrophil" title="Neutrophil">neutrophils</a>, <a href="/wiki/Lymphocyte" title="Lymphocyte">lymphocytes</a>, <a href="/wiki/Eosinophil" title="Eosinophil">eosinophils</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Macrophage" title="Macrophage">macrophages</a> to the initial site. The reaction is usually seen 2–24 hours after the original reaction.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Effector_and_potential_immunoregulatory_roles_of_mast_cells_in_IgE-associated_acquired_immune_responses_76-0"><a href="#cite_note-Effector_and_potential_immunoregulatory_roles_of_mast_cells_in_IgE-associated_acquired_immune_responses-76">[76]</a></sup> Cytokines from mast cells may play a role in the persistence of long-term effects. Late-phase responses seen in asthma are slightly different from those seen in other allergic responses, although they are still caused by release of mediators from eosinophils and are still dependent on activity of T<sub>H</sub>2 cells.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Th2_cytokines_in_the_asthma_late-phase_response_77-0"><a href="#cite_note-Th2_cytokines_in_the_asthma_late-phase_response-77">[77]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Allergic_contact_dermatitis">Allergic contact dermatitis</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Allergy&amp;action=edit&amp;section=17" title="Edit section: Allergic contact dermatitis">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Although <a href="/wiki/Allergic_contact_dermatitis" title="Allergic contact dermatitis">allergic contact dermatitis</a> is termed an "allergic" reaction (which usually refers to type I hypersensitivity), its pathophysiology involves a reaction that more correctly corresponds to a <a href="/wiki/Type_IV_hypersensitivity" title="Type IV hypersensitivity">type IV hypersensitivity</a> reaction.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-78"><a href="#cite_note-78">[78]</a></sup> In type IV hypersensitivity, there is activation of certain types of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/T_cells" title="T cells">T cells</a> (CD8+) that destroy target cells on contact, as well as activated <a href="/wiki/Macrophage" title="Macrophage">macrophages</a> that produce <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hydrolytic_enzyme" title="Hydrolytic enzyme">hydrolytic</a> <a href="/wiki/Enzyme" title="Enzyme">enzymes</a>.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (July 2022)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Diagnosis">Diagnosis</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Allergy&amp;action=edit&amp;section=18" title="Edit section: Diagnosis">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Allergy_testing_machine.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="3000" data-file-width="1993" decoding="async" height="331" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/05/Allergy_testing_machine.jpg/220px-Allergy_testing_machine.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/05/Allergy_testing_machine.jpg/330px-Allergy_testing_machine.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/05/Allergy_testing_machine.jpg/440px-Allergy_testing_machine.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Allergy_testing_machine.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>An allergy testing machine being operated in a diagnostic immunology lab</div></div></div>
<p>Effective management of allergic diseases relies on the ability to make an accurate diagnosis.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-79"><a href="#cite_note-79">[79]</a></sup> Allergy testing can help confirm or rule out allergies.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ReferenceA_80-0"><a href="#cite_note-ReferenceA-80">[80]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ReferenceB_81-0"><a href="#cite_note-ReferenceB-81">[81]</a></sup> Correct diagnosis, counseling, and avoidance advice based on valid allergy test results reduce the incidence of symptoms and need for medications, and improve quality of life.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ReferenceA_80-1"><a href="#cite_note-ReferenceA-80">[80]</a></sup> To assess the presence of allergen-specific IgE antibodies, two different methods can be used: a skin prick test, or an allergy <a href="/wiki/Blood_test" title="Blood test">blood test</a>. Both methods are recommended, and they have similar diagnostic value.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ReferenceB_81-1"><a href="#cite_note-ReferenceB-81">[81]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-82"><a href="#cite_note-82">[82]</a></sup>
</p><p>Skin prick tests and blood tests are equally cost-effective, and health economic evidence shows that both tests were cost-effective compared with no test.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ReferenceA_80-2"><a href="#cite_note-ReferenceA-80">[80]</a></sup> Early and more accurate diagnoses save cost due to reduced consultations, referrals to secondary care, misdiagnosis, and emergency admissions.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-83"><a href="#cite_note-83">[83]</a></sup>
</p><p>Allergy undergoes dynamic changes over time. Regular allergy testing of relevant allergens provides information on if and how patient management can be changed to improve health and quality of life. Annual testing is often the practice for determining whether allergy to milk, egg, soy, and wheat have been outgrown, and the testing interval is extended to 2–3 years for allergy to peanut, tree nuts, fish, and crustacean shellfish.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ReferenceB_81-2"><a href="#cite_note-ReferenceB-81">[81]</a></sup> Results of follow-up testing can guide decision-making regarding whether and when it is safe to introduce or re-introduce allergenic food into the diet.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-United_States_2010_84-0"><a href="#cite_note-United_States_2010-84">[84]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Skin_prick_testing">Skin prick testing</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Allergy&amp;action=edit&amp;section=19" title="Edit section: Skin prick testing">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Allergy_skin_testing.JPG"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="600" data-file-width="800" decoding="async" height="165" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d7/Allergy_skin_testing.JPG/220px-Allergy_skin_testing.JPG" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d7/Allergy_skin_testing.JPG/330px-Allergy_skin_testing.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d7/Allergy_skin_testing.JPG/440px-Allergy_skin_testing.JPG 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Allergy_skin_testing.JPG" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Skin testing on arm</div></div></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Skintest2.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="411" data-file-width="565" decoding="async" height="160" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/89/Skintest2.jpg/220px-Skintest2.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/89/Skintest2.jpg/330px-Skintest2.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/89/Skintest2.jpg/440px-Skintest2.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Skintest2.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Skin testing on back</div></div></div>
<p><a href="/wiki/Skin_test" title="Skin test">Skin testing</a> is also known as "puncture testing" and "prick testing" due to the series of tiny punctures or pricks made into the patient's skin. Tiny amounts of suspected allergens and/or their <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Extracts" title="Extracts">extracts</a> (<i>e.g.</i>, pollen, grass, mite proteins, peanut extract) are introduced to sites on the skin marked with pen or dye (the ink/dye should be carefully selected, lest it cause an allergic response itself). A small plastic or metal device is used to puncture or prick the skin. Sometimes, the allergens are injected "intradermally" into the patient's skin, with a needle and syringe. Common areas for testing include the inside forearm and the back.
</p><p>If the patient is allergic to the substance, then a visible inflammatory reaction will usually occur within 30 minutes. This response will range from slight reddening of the skin to a full-blown <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Urticaria" title="Urticaria">hive</a> (called "wheal and flare") in more sensitive patients similar to a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Mosquito_bite" title="Mosquito bite">mosquito bite</a>. Interpretation of the results of the skin prick test is normally done by allergists on a scale of severity, with +/− meaning borderline reactivity, and 4+ being a large reaction. Increasingly, allergists are measuring and recording the diameter of the wheal and flare reaction. Interpretation by well-trained allergists is often guided by relevant literature.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pmid16164451_85-0"><a href="#cite_note-pmid16164451-85">[85]</a></sup> Some patients may believe they have determined their own allergic sensitivity from observation, but a skin test has been shown to be much better than patient observation to detect allergy.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pmid11101180_86-0"><a href="#cite_note-pmid11101180-86">[86]</a></sup>
</p><p>If a serious life-threatening anaphylactic reaction has brought a patient in for evaluation, some allergists will prefer an initial blood test prior to performing the skin prick test. Skin tests may not be an option if the patient has widespread skin disease or has taken <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Antihistamines" title="Antihistamines">antihistamines</a> in the last several days.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Patch_testing">Patch testing</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Allergy&amp;action=edit&amp;section=20" title="Edit section: Patch testing">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Patch_test" title="Patch test">Patch test</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Epikutanni-test.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1200" data-file-width="1600" decoding="async" height="165" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4c/Epikutanni-test.jpg/220px-Epikutanni-test.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4c/Epikutanni-test.jpg/330px-Epikutanni-test.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4c/Epikutanni-test.jpg/440px-Epikutanni-test.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Epikutanni-test.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Patch_test" title="Patch test">Patch test</a></div></div></div>
<p>Patch testing is a method used to determine if a specific substance causes allergic inflammation of the skin. It tests for delayed reactions. It is used to help ascertain the cause of skin contact allergy or <a href="/wiki/Contact_dermatitis" title="Contact dermatitis">contact dermatitis</a>. Adhesive patches, usually treated with several common allergic chemicals or skin sensitizers, are applied to the back. The skin is then examined for possible local reactions at least twice, usually at 48 hours after application of the patch, and again two or three days later.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Blood_testing">Blood testing</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Allergy&amp;action=edit&amp;section=21" title="Edit section: Blood testing">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>An allergy <a href="/wiki/Blood_test" title="Blood test">blood test</a> is quick and simple and can be ordered by a licensed health care provider (<i>e.g.</i>, an allergy specialist) or general practitioner. Unlike skin-prick testing, a blood test can be performed irrespective of age, skin condition, medication, symptom, disease activity, and pregnancy. Adults and children of any age can get an allergy blood test. For babies and very young children, a single needle stick for allergy blood testing is often gentler than several skin pricks.
</p><p>An allergy blood test is available through most <a href="/wiki/Medical_laboratory" title="Medical laboratory">laboratories</a>. A sample of the patient's blood is sent to a laboratory for analysis, and the results are sent back a few days later. Multiple allergens can be detected with a single blood sample. Allergy blood tests are very safe since the person is not exposed to any allergens during the testing procedure.
</p><p>The test measures the concentration of specific <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/IgE" title="IgE">IgE antibodies</a> in the blood. <a href="/wiki/Quantitative_analysis_(chemistry)" title="Quantitative analysis (chemistry)">Quantitative</a> IgE test results increase the possibility of ranking how different substances may affect symptoms. A rule of thumb is that the higher the IgE antibody value, the greater the likelihood of symptoms. Allergens found at low levels that today do not result in symptoms cannot help predict future symptom development. The quantitative allergy blood result can help determine what a patient is allergic to, help predict and follow the disease development, estimate the risk of a severe reaction, and explain <a href="/wiki/Cross-reactivity" title="Cross-reactivity">cross-reactivity</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-87"><a href="#cite_note-87">[87]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-88"><a href="#cite_note-88">[88]</a></sup>
</p><p>A low total IgE level is not adequate to rule out <a href="/wiki/Sensitization_(immunology)" title="Sensitization (immunology)">sensitization</a> to commonly inhaled allergens.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pmid12911420_89-0"><a href="#cite_note-pmid12911420-89">[89]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Statistics" title="Statistics">Statistical methods</a>, such as <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/ROC_curve" title="ROC curve">ROC curves</a>, predictive value calculations, and likelihood ratios have been used to examine the relationship of various testing methods to each other. These methods have shown that patients with a high total IgE have a high probability of allergic sensitization, but further investigation with allergy tests for specific IgE antibodies for a carefully chosen of allergens is often warranted.
</p><p>Laboratory methods to measure specific IgE antibodies for allergy testing include <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Enzyme-linked_immunosorbent_assay" title="Enzyme-linked immunosorbent assay">enzyme-linked immunosorbent assay</a> (ELISA, or EIA),<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-webmd_90-0"><a href="#cite_note-webmd-90">[90]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Radioallergosorbent_test" title="Radioallergosorbent test">radioallergosorbent test</a> (RAST)<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-webmd_90-1"><a href="#cite_note-webmd-90">[90]</a></sup> and fluorescent enzyme <a href="/wiki/Immunoassay" title="Immunoassay">immunoassay</a> (FEIA).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-KhanUeno-Yamanouchi2012_91-0"><a href="#cite_note-KhanUeno-Yamanouchi2012-91">[91]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Other_testing">Other testing</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Allergy&amp;action=edit&amp;section=22" title="Edit section: Other testing">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p><b>Challenge testing:</b> Challenge testing is when tiny amounts of a suspected allergen are introduced to the body orally, through inhalation, or via other routes. Except for testing food and medication allergies, challenges are rarely performed. When this type of testing is chosen, it must be closely supervised by an <a href="/wiki/Allergist" title="Allergist">allergist</a>.
</p><p><b>Elimination/challenge tests:</b> This testing method is used most often with foods or medicines. A patient with a suspected allergen is instructed to modify his diet to totally avoid that allergen for a set time. If the patient experiences significant improvement, he may then be "challenged" by reintroducing the allergen, to see if symptoms are reproduced.
</p><p><b>Unreliable tests:</b> There are other types of allergy testing methods that are unreliable, including <a href="/wiki/Applied_kinesiology" title="Applied kinesiology">applied kinesiology</a> (allergy testing through muscle relaxation), <a href="/wiki/Cytotoxicity" title="Cytotoxicity">cytotoxicity</a> testing, urine autoinjection, skin <a href="/wiki/Titration" title="Titration">titration</a> (Rinkel method), and provocative and neutralization (subcutaneous) testing or sublingual provocation.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Allergy_Diagnosis_92-0"><a href="#cite_note-Allergy_Diagnosis-92">[92]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Differential_diagnosis">Differential diagnosis</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Allergy&amp;action=edit&amp;section=23" title="Edit section: Differential diagnosis">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Before a diagnosis of allergic disease can be confirmed, other plausible causes of the presenting symptoms should be considered.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-93"><a href="#cite_note-93">[93]</a></sup> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Vasomotor_rhinitis" title="Vasomotor rhinitis">Vasomotor rhinitis</a>, for example, is one of many illnesses that share symptoms with allergic rhinitis, underscoring the need for professional differential diagnosis.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pmid16190503_94-0"><a href="#cite_note-pmid16190503-94">[94]</a></sup> Once a diagnosis of <a href="/wiki/Asthma" title="Asthma">asthma</a>, rhinitis, anaphylaxis, or other allergic disease has been made, there are several methods for discovering the causative agent of that allergy.
</p>
