<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Prevention">Prevention</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Allergy&amp;action=edit&amp;section=24" title="Edit section: Prevention">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Further information: <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Allergy_prevention_in_children" title="Allergy prevention in children">Allergy prevention in children</a></div>
<p>Giving peanut products early may decrease the risk of allergies while only <a href="/wiki/Breastfeeding" title="Breastfeeding">breastfeeding</a> during at least the first few months of life may decrease the risk of <a href="/wiki/Dermatitis" title="Dermatitis">dermatitis</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Gre2019_95-0"><a href="#cite_note-Gre2019-95">[95]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Gar2018_96-0"><a href="#cite_note-Gar2018-96">[96]</a></sup> There is no good evidence that a mother's diet during <a href="/wiki/Pregnancy" title="Pregnancy">pregnancy</a> or breastfeeding affects the risk of allergies,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Gre2019_95-1"><a href="#cite_note-Gre2019-95">[95]</a></sup> nor is there evidence that delayed introduction of certain foods is useful.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Gre2019_95-2"><a href="#cite_note-Gre2019-95">[95]</a></sup> Early exposure to potential allergens may actually be protective.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Sic2014_5-3"><a href="#cite_note-Sic2014-5">[5]</a></sup>
</p><p>Fish oil supplementation during pregnancy is associated with a lower risk.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Gar2018_96-1"><a href="#cite_note-Gar2018-96">[96]</a></sup> Probiotic supplements during pregnancy or infancy may help to prevent atopic dermatitis.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-97"><a href="#cite_note-97">[97]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-98"><a href="#cite_note-98">[98]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Management">Management</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Allergy&amp;action=edit&amp;section=25" title="Edit section: Management">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>Management of allergies typically involves avoiding the allergy trigger and taking medications to improve the symptoms.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NIH2015Imm_6-5"><a href="#cite_note-NIH2015Imm-6">[6]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Allergen_immunotherapy" title="Allergen immunotherapy">Allergen immunotherapy</a> may be useful for some types of allergies.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NIH2015Imm_6-6"><a href="#cite_note-NIH2015Imm-6">[6]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Medication">Medication</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Allergy&amp;action=edit&amp;section=26" title="Edit section: Medication">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Several medications may be used to block the action of allergic mediators, or to prevent activation of cells and <a href="/wiki/Degranulation" title="Degranulation">degranulation</a> processes. These include <a href="/wiki/Antihistamine" title="Antihistamine">antihistamines</a>, <a href="/wiki/Glucocorticoid" title="Glucocorticoid">glucocorticoids</a>, <a href="/wiki/Epinephrine_(medication)" title="Epinephrine (medication)">epinephrine</a> (adrenaline), <a href="/wiki/Mast_cell_stabilizer" title="Mast cell stabilizer">mast cell stabilizers</a>, and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Antileukotriene_agent" title="Antileukotriene agent">antileukotriene agents</a> are common treatments of allergic diseases.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-MCAS_99-0"><a href="#cite_note-MCAS-99">[99]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Anticholinergic" title="Anticholinergic">Anticholinergics</a>, <a href="/wiki/Decongestant" title="Decongestant">decongestants</a>, and other compounds thought to impair <a href="/wiki/Eosinophil" title="Eosinophil">eosinophil</a> <a href="/wiki/Chemotaxis" title="Chemotaxis">chemotaxis</a> are also commonly used. Although rare, the severity of anaphylaxis often requires <a href="/wiki/Epinephrine_(medication)" title="Epinephrine (medication)">epinephrine</a> injection, and where medical care is unavailable, a device known as an <a href="/wiki/Epinephrine_autoinjector" title="Epinephrine autoinjector">epinephrine autoinjector</a> may be used.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-tang03_27-2"><a href="#cite_note-tang03-27">[27]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Immunotherapy">Immunotherapy</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Allergy&amp;action=edit&amp;section=27" title="Edit section: Immunotherapy">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Allergen_immunotherapy" title="Allergen immunotherapy">Allergen immunotherapy</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Anti-Allergy_Immunotherapy.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1552" data-file-width="2083" decoding="async" height="164" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/ad/Anti-Allergy_Immunotherapy.jpg/220px-Anti-Allergy_Immunotherapy.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/ad/Anti-Allergy_Immunotherapy.jpg/330px-Anti-Allergy_Immunotherapy.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/ad/Anti-Allergy_Immunotherapy.jpg/440px-Anti-Allergy_Immunotherapy.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Anti-Allergy_Immunotherapy.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Anti-allergy immunotherapy</div></div></div>
<p>Allergen <a href="/wiki/Immunotherapy" title="Immunotherapy">immunotherapy</a> is useful for environmental allergies, allergies to insect bites, and asthma.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NIH2015Imm_6-7"><a href="#cite_note-NIH2015Imm-6">[6]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Abra2010_100-0"><a href="#cite_note-Abra2010-100">[100]</a></sup> Its benefit for food allergies is unclear and thus not recommended.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NIH2015Imm_6-8"><a href="#cite_note-NIH2015Imm-6">[6]</a></sup> Immunotherapy involves exposing people to larger and larger amounts of allergen in an effort to change the immune system's response.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NIH2015Imm_6-9"><a href="#cite_note-NIH2015Imm-6">[6]</a></sup>
</p><p>Meta-analyses have found that injections of allergens under the skin is effective in the treatment in allergic rhinitis in children<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Penagos06_101-0"><a href="#cite_note-Penagos06-101">[101]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-102"><a href="#cite_note-102">[102]</a></sup> and in asthma.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Abra2010_100-1"><a href="#cite_note-Abra2010-100">[100]</a></sup> The benefits may last for years after treatment is stopped.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Canonica09_103-0"><a href="#cite_note-Canonica09-103">[103]</a></sup> It is generally safe and effective for allergic rhinitis and <a href="/wiki/Allergic_conjunctivitis" title="Allergic conjunctivitis">conjunctivitis</a>, allergic forms of asthma, and stinging insects.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pmid17803880_104-0"><a href="#cite_note-pmid17803880-104">[104]</a></sup>
</p><p>To a lesser extent, the evidence also supports the use of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sublingual_immunotherapy" title="Sublingual immunotherapy">sublingual immunotherapy</a> for rhinitis and asthma.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Canonica09_103-1"><a href="#cite_note-Canonica09-103">[103]</a></sup> For seasonal allergies the benefit is small.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-105"><a href="#cite_note-105">[105]</a></sup> In this form the allergen is given under the tongue and people often prefer it to injections.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Canonica09_103-2"><a href="#cite_note-Canonica09-103">[103]</a></sup> Immunotherapy is not recommended as a stand-alone treatment for asthma.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Canonica09_103-3"><a href="#cite_note-Canonica09-103">[103]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Alternative_medicine">Alternative medicine</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Allergy&amp;action=edit&amp;section=28" title="Edit section: Alternative medicine">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>An experimental treatment, <a href="/wiki/Enzyme_potentiated_desensitization" title="Enzyme potentiated desensitization">enzyme potentiated desensitization</a> (EPD), has been tried for decades but is not generally accepted as effective.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pmid15042943_106-0"><a href="#cite_note-pmid15042943-106">[106]</a></sup> EPD uses dilutions of allergen and an enzyme, <a href="/wiki/Beta-glucuronidase" title="Beta-glucuronidase">beta-glucuronidase</a>, to which <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/T-lymphocytes,_regulatory" title="T-lymphocytes, regulatory">T-regulatory lymphocytes</a> are supposed to respond by favoring desensitization, or down-regulation, rather than sensitization. EPD has also been tried for the treatment of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Autoimmune_diseases" title="Autoimmune diseases">autoimmune diseases</a>, but evidence does not show effectiveness.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pmid15042943_106-1"><a href="#cite_note-pmid15042943-106">[106]</a></sup>
</p><p>A review found no effectiveness of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Homeopathic_treatment" title="Homeopathic treatment">homeopathic treatments</a> and no difference compared with <a href="/wiki/Placebo" title="Placebo">placebo</a>. The authors concluded that based on rigorous clinical trials of all types of homeopathy for childhood and adolescence ailments, there is no convincing evidence that supports the use of homeopathic treatments.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pmid17285788_107-0"><a href="#cite_note-pmid17285788-107">[107]</a></sup>
</p><p>According to the <a href="/wiki/National_Center_for_Complementary_and_Integrative_Health" title="National Center for Complementary and Integrative Health">National Center for Complementary and Integrative Health</a>, U.S., the evidence is relatively strong that <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Saline_nasal_irrigation" title="Saline nasal irrigation">saline nasal irrigation</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Butterbur" title="Butterbur">butterbur</a> are effective, when compared to other <a href="/wiki/Alternative_medicine" title="Alternative medicine">alternative medicine</a> treatments, for which the scientific evidence is weak, negative, or nonexistent, such as honey, acupuncture, omega 3's, probiotics, astragalus, capsaicin, grape seed extract, Pycnogenol, quercetin, spirulina, stinging nettle, tinospora, or guduchi.
<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-108"><a href="#cite_note-108">[108]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-109"><a href="#cite_note-109">[109]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Epidemiology">Epidemiology</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Allergy&amp;action=edit&amp;section=29" title="Edit section: Epidemiology">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>The allergic diseases—hay fever and asthma—have increased in the <a href="/wiki/Western_world" title="Western world">Western world</a> over the past 2–3 decades.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Platts_110-0"><a href="#cite_note-Platts-110">[110]</a></sup> Increases in allergic asthma and other atopic disorders in industrialized nations, it is estimated, began in the 1960s and 1970s, with further increases occurring during the 1980s and 1990s,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Bloomfield_111-0"><a href="#cite_note-Bloomfield-111">[111]</a></sup> although some suggest that a steady rise in sensitization has been occurring since the 1920s.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-The_allergy_epidemic_extends_beyond_the_past_few_decades_112-0"><a href="#cite_note-The_allergy_epidemic_extends_beyond_the_past_few_decades-112">[112]</a></sup> The number of new cases per year of atopy in developing countries has, in general, remained much lower.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Bloomfield_111-1"><a href="#cite_note-Bloomfield-111">[111]</a></sup>
</p>

<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Changing_frequency">Changing frequency</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Allergy&amp;action=edit&amp;section=30" title="Edit section: Changing frequency">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Although genetic factors govern susceptibility to atopic disease, increases in <a href="/wiki/Atopy" title="Atopy">atopy</a> have occurred within too short a period to be explained by a genetic change in the population, thus pointing to environmental or lifestyle changes.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Bloomfield_111-2"><a href="#cite_note-Bloomfield-111">[111]</a></sup> Several hypotheses have been identified to explain this increased rate. Increased exposure to perennial allergens may be due to housing changes and increased time spent indoors, and a decreased activation of a common immune control mechanism may be caused by changes in cleanliness or hygiene, and exacerbated by dietary changes, obesity, and decline in physical exercise.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Platts_110-1"><a href="#cite_note-Platts-110">[110]</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Hygiene_hypothesis" title="Hygiene hypothesis">hygiene hypothesis</a> maintains<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Hay_fever,_hygiene,_and_household_size_124-0"><a href="#cite_note-Hay_fever,_hygiene,_and_household_size-124">[124]</a></sup> that high living standards and hygienic conditions exposes children to fewer infections. It is thought that reduced bacterial and viral infections early in life direct the maturing immune system away from <a href="/wiki/T_helper_cell" title="T helper cell">T<sub>H</sub></a>1 type responses, leading to unrestrained T<sub>H</sub>2 responses that allow for an increase in allergy.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Yazdanbakhsh02_73-1"><a href="#cite_note-Yazdanbakhsh02-73">[73]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Renz_125-0"><a href="#cite_note-Renz-125">[125]</a></sup>
</p><p>Changes in rates and types of infection alone, however, have been unable to explain the observed increase in allergic disease, and recent evidence has focused attention on the importance of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Gut_flora" title="Gut flora">gastrointestinal microbial environment</a>. Evidence has shown that exposure to food and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Fecal-oral_route" title="Fecal-oral route">fecal-oral</a> pathogens, such as <a href="/wiki/Hepatitis_A" title="Hepatitis A">hepatitis A</a>, <i><a href="/wiki/Toxoplasma_gondii" title="Toxoplasma gondii">Toxoplasma gondii</a></i>, and <i><a href="/wiki/Helicobacter_pylori" title="Helicobacter pylori">Helicobacter pylori</a></i> (which also tend to be more prevalent in developing countries), can reduce the overall risk of atopy by more than 60%,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Exposure_to_foodborne_and_orofecal_microbes_versus_airborne_viruses_in_relation_to_atopy_and_allergic_asthma:_epidemiological_study_126-0"><a href="#cite_note-Exposure_to_foodborne_and_orofecal_microbes_versus_airborne_viruses_in_relation_to_atopy_and_allergic_asthma:_epidemiological_study-126">[126]</a></sup> and an increased rate of parasitic infections has been associated with a decreased prevalence of asthma.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Parasites_and_asthma--predictive_or_protective?_127-0"><a href="#cite_note-Parasites_and_asthma--predictive_or_protective?-127">[127]</a></sup> It is speculated that these infections exert their effect by critically altering T<sub>H</sub>1/T<sub>H</sub>2 regulation.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Sheikh_128-0"><a href="#cite_note-Sheikh-128">[128]</a></sup> Important elements of newer hygiene hypotheses also include exposure to <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Endotoxin" title="Endotoxin">endotoxins</a>, exposure to <a href="/wiki/Pet" title="Pet">pets</a> and growing up on a farm.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Sheikh_128-1"><a href="#cite_note-Sheikh-128">[128]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="History">History</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Allergy&amp;action=edit&amp;section=31" title="Edit section: History">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>Some symptoms attributable to allergic diseases are mentioned in ancient sources.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-aai_129-0"><a href="#cite_note-aai-129">[129]</a></sup> Particularly, three members of the Roman <a href="/wiki/Julio-Claudian_dynasty" title="Julio-Claudian dynasty">Julio-Claudian dynasty</a> (<a href="/wiki/Augustus" title="Augustus">Augustus</a>, <a href="/wiki/Claudius" title="Claudius">Claudius</a> and <a href="/wiki/Britannicus" title="Britannicus">Britannicus</a>) are suspected to have a family history of atopy.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-aai_129-1"><a href="#cite_note-aai-129">[129]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-130"><a href="#cite_note-130">[130]</a></sup> The concept of "allergy" was originally introduced in 1906 by the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Vienna,_Austria" title="Vienna, Austria">Viennese</a> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Pediatrician" title="Pediatrician">pediatrician</a> <a href="/wiki/Clemens_von_Pirquet" title="Clemens von Pirquet">Clemens von Pirquet</a>, after he noticed that patients who had received injections of horse serum or smallpox vaccine usually had quicker, more severe reactions to second injections.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-131"><a href="#cite_note-131">[131]</a></sup> Pirquet called this phenomenon "allergy" from the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ancient_Greek_language" title="Ancient Greek language">Ancient Greek</a> words <a class="extiw" href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/%E1%BC%84%CE%BB%CE%BB%CE%BF%CF%82" title="wikt:ἄλλος">ἄλλος</a> <i>allos</i> meaning "other" and <a class="extiw" href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/%E1%BC%94%CF%81%CE%B3%CE%BF%CE%BD" title="wikt:ἔργον">ἔργον</a> <i>ergon</i> meaning "work".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Allergie_132-0"><a href="#cite_note-Allergie-132">[132]</a></sup>
</p><p>All forms of hypersensitivity used to be classified as allergies, and all were thought to be caused by an improper activation of the immune system. Later, it became clear that several different <a href="/wiki/Disease" title="Disease">disease</a> mechanisms were implicated, with a common link to a disordered activation of the immune system. In 1963, a new classification scheme was designed by <a href="/wiki/Philip_George_Houthem_Gell" title="Philip George Houthem Gell">Philip Gell</a> and <a href="/wiki/Robin_Coombs" title="Robin Coombs">Robin Coombs</a> that described four types of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hypersensitivities" title="Hypersensitivities">hypersensitivity reactions</a>, known as Type I to Type IV hypersensitivity.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-GellCoombs_133-0"><a href="#cite_note-GellCoombs-133">[133]</a></sup> With this new classification, the word <i>allergy</i>, sometimes clarified as a <i>true allergy</i>, was restricted to type I hypersensitivities (also called immediate hypersensitivity), which are characterized as rapidly developing reactions involving IgE antibodies.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-134"><a href="#cite_note-134">[134]</a></sup>
</p><p>A major breakthrough in understanding the mechanisms of allergy was the discovery of the antibody class labeled <a href="/wiki/Immunoglobulin_E" title="Immunoglobulin E">immunoglobulin E</a> (IgE). IgE was simultaneously discovered in 1966–67 by two independent groups:<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-135"><a href="#cite_note-135">[135]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Kimishige_Ishizaka" title="Kimishige Ishizaka">Ishizaka</a>'s team at the Children's Asthma Research Institute and Hospital in Denver, USA,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Ishizaka_K_136-0"><a href="#cite_note-Ishizaka_K-136">[136]</a></sup> and by Gunnar Johansson and Hans Bennich in Uppsala, Sweden.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-137"><a href="#cite_note-137">[137]</a></sup> Their joint paper was published in April 1969.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Joint_paper_1969_138-0"><a href="#cite_note-Joint_paper_1969-138">[138]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Diagnosis_2">Diagnosis</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Allergy&amp;action=edit&amp;section=32" title="Edit section: Diagnosis">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Radiometric assays include the <a href="/wiki/Radioallergosorbent_test" title="Radioallergosorbent test">radioallergosorbent test</a> (RAST test) method, which uses IgE-binding (anti-IgE) antibodies labeled with <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Radioactive_isotope" title="Radioactive isotope">radioactive isotopes</a> for quantifying the levels of IgE antibody in the blood.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pmid7630219_139-0"><a href="#cite_note-pmid7630219-139">[139]</a></sup> Other, newer methods use colorimetric or fluorescence-labeled technology in the place of radioactive isotopes.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (April 2014)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup>
</p><p>The RAST methodology was invented and marketed in 1974 by Pharmacia Diagnostics AB, Uppsala, Sweden, and the acronym RAST is actually a brand name. In 1989, Pharmacia Diagnostics AB replaced it with a superior test named the ImmunoCAP Specific IgE blood test, which uses the newer fluorescence-labeled technology.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (April 2014)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup>
</p><p><a href="/wiki/American_College_of_Allergy,_Asthma_and_Immunology" title="American College of Allergy, Asthma and Immunology">American College of Allergy Asthma and Immunology</a> (ACAAI) and the <a href="/wiki/American_Academy_of_Allergy,_Asthma,_and_Immunology" title="American Academy of Allergy, Asthma, and Immunology">American Academy of Allergy Asthma and Immunology</a> (AAAAI) issued the Joint Task Force Report "Pearls and pitfalls of allergy diagnostic testing" in 2008, and is firm in its statement that the term RAST is now obsolete:
</p>
<style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r996844942">.mw-parser-output .templatequote{overflow:hidden;margin:1em 0;padding:0 40px}.mw-parser-output .templatequote .templatequotecite{line-height:1.5em;text-align:left;padding-left:1.6em;margin-top:0}</style><blockquote class="templatequote"><p>The term RAST became a colloquialism for all varieties of (in vitro allergy) tests.  This is unfortunate because it is well recognized that there are well-performing tests and some that do not perform so well, yet they are all called RASTs, making it difficult to distinguish which is which. For these reasons, it is now recommended that use of RAST as a generic descriptor of these tests be abandoned.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Cox2008_14-1"><a href="#cite_note-Cox2008-14">[14]</a></sup></p></blockquote>
<p>The updated version, the ImmunoCAP Specific IgE blood test, is the only specific IgE assay to receive <a href="/wiki/Food_and_Drug_Administration" title="Food and Drug Administration">Food and Drug Administration</a> approval to quantitatively report to its detection limit of 0.1kU/L.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (April 2014)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Medical_specialty">Medical specialty</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Allergy&amp;action=edit&amp;section=33" title="Edit section: Medical specialty">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1066479718" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><table class="infobox"><caption class="infobox-title">Allergist/Immunologist</caption><tbody><tr><th class="infobox-header" colspan="2">Occupation</th></tr><tr><th class="infobox-label" scope="row">Names</th><td class="infobox-data">
<ul><li>Physician</li></ul>
</td></tr><tr><th class="infobox-label" scope="row"><div style="display: inline-block; line-height: 1.2em; padding: .1em 0;">Occupation type</div></th><td class="infobox-data"><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Specialty_(medicine)" title="Specialty (medicine)">Specialty</a></td></tr><tr><th class="infobox-label" scope="row"><div style="display: inline-block; line-height: 1.2em; padding: .1em 0;">Activity sectors</div></th><td class="infobox-data"><a href="/wiki/Medicine" title="Medicine">Medicine</a></td></tr><tr><th class="infobox-label" scope="row">Specialty</th><td class="infobox-data"><span><span><a href="/wiki/Immunology" title="Immunology">immunology</a></span></span></td></tr><tr><th class="infobox-header" colspan="2">Description</th></tr><tr><th class="infobox-label" scope="row"><div style="display: inline-block; line-height: 1.2em; padding: .1em 0;">Education required</div></th><td class="infobox-data">
<ul><li><a href="/wiki/Doctor_of_Medicine" title="Doctor of Medicine">Doctor of Medicine</a> (M.D.)</li>
<li><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Doctor_of_Osteopathic_medicine" title="Doctor of Osteopathic medicine">Doctor of Osteopathic medicine</a> (D.O.)</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Bachelor_of_Medicine,_Bachelor_of_Surgery" title="Bachelor of Medicine, Bachelor of Surgery">Bachelor of Medicine, Bachelor of Surgery</a> (M.B.B.S.)</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Bachelor_of_Medicine,_Bachelor_of_Surgery" title="Bachelor of Medicine, Bachelor of Surgery">Bachelor of Medicine, Bachelor of Surgery</a> (MBChB)</li></ul>
</td></tr><tr><th class="infobox-label" scope="row"><div style="display: inline-block; line-height: 1.2em; padding: .1em 0;">Fields of<br/>employment</div></th><td class="infobox-data"><a href="/wiki/Hospital" title="Hospital">Hospitals</a>, <a href="/wiki/Clinic" title="Clinic">Clinics</a></td></tr></tbody></table>
<p>An allergist is a physician specially trained to manage and treat allergies, asthma, and the other allergic diseases.
In the United States physicians holding certification by the <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=American_Board_of_Allergy_and_Immunology&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="American Board of Allergy and Immunology (page does not exist)">American Board of Allergy and Immunology</a> (ABAI) have successfully completed an accredited educational program and evaluation process, including a proctored examination to demonstrate knowledge, skills, and experience in patient care in allergy and immunology.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ABAI:_American_Board_of_Allergy_and_Immunology_140-0"><a href="#cite_note-ABAI:_American_Board_of_Allergy_and_Immunology-140">[140]</a></sup> Becoming an allergist/immunologist requires completion of at least nine years of training. After completing medical school and graduating with a medical degree, a physician will undergo three years of training in <a href="/wiki/Internal_medicine" title="Internal medicine">internal medicine</a> (to become an internist) or <a href="/wiki/Pediatrics" title="Pediatrics">pediatrics</a> (to become a pediatrician). Once physicians have finished training in one of these specialties, they must pass the exam of either the <a href="/wiki/American_Board_of_Pediatrics" title="American Board of Pediatrics">American Board of Pediatrics</a> (ABP), the <a href="/wiki/American_Osteopathic_Board_of_Pediatrics" title="American Osteopathic Board of Pediatrics">American Osteopathic Board of Pediatrics</a> (AOBP), the <a href="/wiki/American_Board_of_Internal_Medicine" title="American Board of Internal Medicine">American Board of Internal Medicine</a> (ABIM), or the <a href="/wiki/American_Osteopathic_Board_of_Internal_Medicine" title="American Osteopathic Board of Internal Medicine">American Osteopathic Board of Internal Medicine</a> (AOBIM). Internists or pediatricians wishing to focus on the sub-specialty of allergy-immunology then complete at least an additional two years of study, called a fellowship, in an allergy/immunology training program. Allergist/immunologists listed as ABAI-certified have successfully passed the certifying examination of the ABAI following their fellowship.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-AAAAI_-_What_is_an_Allergist?_141-0"><a href="#cite_note-AAAAI_-_What_is_an_Allergist?-141">[141]</a></sup>
</p><p>In the United Kingdom, allergy is a subspecialty of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/General_medicine" title="General medicine">general medicine</a> or <a href="/wiki/Pediatrics" title="Pediatrics">pediatrics</a>. After obtaining postgraduate exams (<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Membership_of_the_Royal_College_of_Physicians" title="Membership of the Royal College of Physicians">MRCP</a> or <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Membership_of_the_Royal_College_of_Paediatrics_and_Child_Health&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Membership of the Royal College of Paediatrics and Child Health (page does not exist)">MRCPCH</a>), a doctor works for several years as a <a href="/wiki/Specialist_registrar" title="Specialist registrar">specialist registrar</a> before qualifying for the <a href="/wiki/General_Medical_Council" title="General Medical Council">General Medical Council</a> specialist register. Allergy services may also be delivered by <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Immunologist" title="Immunologist">immunologists</a>. A 2003 <a href="/wiki/Royal_College_of_Physicians" title="Royal College of Physicians">Royal College of Physicians</a> report presented a case for improvement of what were felt to be inadequate allergy services in the UK.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-142"><a href="#cite_note-142">[142]</a></sup> In 2006, the <a href="/wiki/House_of_Lords" title="House of Lords">House of Lords</a> convened a subcommittee. It concluded likewise in 2007 that allergy services were insufficient to deal with what the Lords referred to as an "allergy epidemic" and its social cost; it made several recommendations.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Allergy_-_HL_166-I,_6th_Report_of_Session_2006-07_-_Volume_1:_Report_143-0"><a href="#cite_note-Allergy_-_HL_166-I,_6th_Report_of_Session_2006-07_-_Volume_1:_Report-143">[143]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Research">Research</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Allergy&amp;action=edit&amp;section=34" title="Edit section: Research">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>Low-allergen foods are being developed, as are improvements in skin prick test predictions; evaluation of the atopy patch test, wasp sting outcomes predictions, a rapidly disintegrating epinephrine tablet, and anti-<a href="/wiki/Interleukin_5" title="Interleukin 5">IL-5</a> for eosinophilic diseases.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Advances_in_allergic_skin_disease,_anaphylaxis,_and_hypersensitivity_reactions_to_foods,_drugs,_and_insects_144-0"><a href="#cite_note-Advances_in_allergic_skin_disease,_anaphylaxis,_and_hypersensitivity_reactions_to_foods,_drugs,_and_insects-144">[144]</a></sup>
</p>
